A variation in one of the many genes that contributes to a trait may have only a small effect on the phenotype; together, these genes can produce a continuum of possible phenotypic values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Directionality_of_selection">Directionality of selection</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Directional_selection" title="Directional selection">Directional selection</a></div>
<p>When some component of a trait is heritable, selection alters the frequencies of the different alleles, or variants of the gene that produces the variants of the trait. Selection can be divided into three classes, on the basis of its effect on allele frequencies: <a href="/wiki/Directional_selection" title="Directional selection">directional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stabilizing_selection" title="Stabilizing selection">stabilizing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Disruptive_selection" title="Disruptive selection">disruptive selection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rice_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rice-104">[103]</a></sup> Directional selection occurs when an allele has a greater fitness than others, so that it increases in frequency, gaining an increasing share in the population. This process can continue until the allele is <a href="/wiki/Fixation_(population_genetics)" title="Fixation (population genetics)">fixed</a> and the entire population shares the fitter phenotype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Far more common is stabilizing selection, which lowers the frequency of alleles that have a deleterious effect on the phenotype—that is, produce organisms of lower fitness. This process can continue until the allele is eliminated from the population. Stabilizing selection <a href="/wiki/Conserved_sequence" title="Conserved sequence">conserves</a> functional genetic features, such as <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein-coding genes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Regulatory_sequence" title="Regulatory sequence">regulatory sequences</a>, over time by selective pressure against deleterious variants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Disruptive (or diversifying) selection is selection favoring extreme trait values over intermediate trait values. Disruptive selection may cause <a href="/wiki/Sympatric_speciation" title="Sympatric speciation">sympatric speciation</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niche_partitioning" title="Niche partitioning">niche partitioning</a>.
</p><p>Some forms of <a href="/wiki/Balancing_selection" title="Balancing selection">balancing selection</a> do not result in fixation, but maintain an allele at intermediate frequencies in a population. This can occur in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a> species (with pairs of chromosomes) when <a href="/wiki/Zygosity#Heterozygous" title="Zygosity">heterozygous</a> individuals (with just one copy of the allele) have a higher fitness than homozygous individuals (with two copies). This is called heterozygote advantage or over-dominance, of which the best-known example is the resistance to malaria in humans heterozygous for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_anaemia" title="Sickle-cell anaemia">sickle-cell anaemia</a>. Maintenance of allelic variation can also occur through <a href="/wiki/Disruptive_selection" title="Disruptive selection">disruptive or diversifying selection</a>, which favours genotypes that depart from the average in either direction (that is, the opposite of over-dominance), and can result in a <a href="/wiki/Multimodal_distribution" title="Multimodal distribution">bimodal distribution</a> of trait values. Finally, balancing selection can occur through frequency-dependent selection, where the fitness of one particular phenotype depends on the distribution of other phenotypes in the population. The principles of <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a> have been applied to understand the fitness distributions in these situations, particularly in the study of kin selection and the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Reciprocal_altruism" title="Reciprocal altruism">reciprocal altruism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trivers_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trivers-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Selection.2C_genetic_variation.2C_and_drift"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Selection,_genetic_variation,_and_drift">Selection, genetic variation, and drift</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variation" title="Genetic variation">Genetic variation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">Genetic drift</a></div>
<p>A portion of all genetic variation is functionally neutral, producing no phenotypic effect or significant difference in fitness. <a href="/wiki/Motoo_Kimura" title="Motoo Kimura">Motoo Kimura</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Neutral_theory_of_molecular_evolution" title="Neutral theory of molecular evolution">neutral theory of molecular evolution</a> by <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">genetic drift</a> proposes that this variation accounts for a large fraction of observed genetic diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kimura_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kimura-109">[108]</a></sup> Neutral events can radically reduce genetic variation through <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">population bottlenecks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> which among other things can cause the <a href="/wiki/Founder_effect" title="Founder effect">founder effect</a> in initially small new populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell1996_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell1996-111">[110]</a></sup> When genetic variation does not result in differences in fitness, selection cannot directly affect the frequency of such variation. As a result, the genetic variation at those sites is higher than at sites where variation does influence fitness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rice_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rice-104">[103]</a></sup> However, after a period with no new mutations, the genetic variation at these sites is eliminated due to genetic drift. Natural selection reduces genetic variation by eliminating maladapted individuals, and consequently the mutations that caused the maladaptation. At the same time, new mutations occur, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Mutation%E2%80%93selection_balance" title="Mutation–selection balance">mutation–selection balance</a>. The exact outcome of the two processes depends both on the rate at which new mutations occur and on the strength of the natural selection, which is a function of how unfavourable the mutation proves to be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lynch-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genetic_linkage" title="Genetic linkage">Genetic linkage</a> occurs when the <a href="/wiki/Locus_(genetics)" title="Locus (genetics)">loci</a> of two alleles are in close proximity on a chromosome. During the formation of gametes, recombination reshuffles the alleles. The chance that such a reshuffle occurs between two alleles is inversely related to the distance between them. <a href="/wiki/Selective_sweep" title="Selective sweep">Selective sweeps</a> occur when an allele becomes more common in a population as a result of positive selection. As the prevalence of one allele increases, closely linked alleles can also become more common by "<a href="/wiki/Genetic_hitchhiking" title="Genetic hitchhiking">genetic hitchhiking</a>", whether they are neutral or even slightly deleterious. A strong selective sweep results in a region of the genome where the positively selected <a href="/wiki/Haplotype" title="Haplotype">haplotype</a> (the allele and its neighbours) are in essence the only ones that exist in the population. Selective sweeps can be detected by measuring <a href="/wiki/Linkage_disequilibrium" title="Linkage disequilibrium">linkage disequilibrium</a>, or whether a given haplotype is overrepresented in the population. Since a selective sweep also results in selection of neighbouring alleles, the presence of a block of strong linkage disequilibrium might indicate a 'recent' selective sweep near the centre of the block.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaynardSmithHaigh_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaynardSmithHaigh-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Background_selection" title="Background selection">Background selection</a> is the opposite of a selective sweep. If a specific site experiences strong and persistent purifying selection, linked variation tends to be weeded out along with it, producing a region in the genome of low overall variability. Because background selection is a result of deleterious new mutations, which can occur randomly in any haplotype, it does not produce clear blocks of linkage disequilibrium, although with low recombination it can still lead to slightly negative linkage disequilibrium overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keightley_&amp;_Otto_2006_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keightley_&amp;_Otto_2006-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Universal_Darwinism" title="Universal Darwinism">Universal Darwinism</a></div>
<p>Darwin's ideas, along with those of <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, had a profound influence on 19th century thought, including his radical claim that "elaborately constructed forms, so different from each other, and dependent on each other in so complex a manner" evolved from the simplest forms of life by a few simple principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> This inspired some of Darwin's most ardent supporters—and provoked the strongest opposition. Natural selection had the power, according to <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a>, to "dethrone some of the deepest and most traditional comforts of Western thought", such as the belief that humans have a special place in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the words of the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a>, "Darwin's dangerous idea" of evolution by natural selection is a "universal acid," which cannot be kept restricted to any vessel or container, as it soon leaks out, working its way into ever-wider surroundings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> Thus, in the last decades, the concept of natural selection has spread from <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a> to other disciplines, including <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">evolutionary computation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_Darwinism" title="Quantum Darwinism">quantum Darwinism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_economics" title="Evolutionary economics">evolutionary economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_epistemology" title="Evolutionary epistemology">evolutionary epistemology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology" title="Evolutionary psychology">evolutionary psychology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lee_Smolin#Cosmological_natural_selection" title="Lee Smolin">cosmological natural selection</a>. This unlimited applicability has been called <a href="/wiki/Universal_Darwinism" title="Universal Darwinism">universal Darwinism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_life">Origin of life</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">Abiogenesis</a></div>
<p>How life originated from inorganic matter remains an unresolved problem in biology. One prominent hypothesis is that life first appeared <a href="/wiki/RNA_world" title="RNA world">in the form of short self-replicating RNA</a> polymers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> On this view, life may have come into existence when <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> chains first experienced the basic conditions, as conceived by Charles Darwin, for natural selection to operate. These conditions are: heritability, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variability" title="Genetic variability">variation of type</a>, and competition for limited resources. The fitness of an early <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis#RNA_synthesis_and_replication" title="Abiogenesis">RNA replicator</a> would likely have been a function of adaptive capacities that were intrinsic (i.e., determined by the <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_sequence" title="Nucleic acid sequence">nucleotide sequence</a>) and the availability of resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michod_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michod-121">[120]</a></sup> The three primary adaptive capacities could logically have been: (1) the capacity to replicate with moderate fidelity (giving rise to both heritability and variation of type), (2) the capacity to avoid decay, and (3) the capacity to acquire and process resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michod_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michod-121">[120]</a></sup> These capacities would have been determined initially by the folded configurations (including those configurations with <a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozyme</a> activity) of the RNA replicators that, in turn, would have been encoded in their individual nucleotide sequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell_and_molecular_biology">Cell and molecular biology</span></h3>
<p>In 1881, the embryologist <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Roux" title="Wilhelm Roux">Wilhelm Roux</a> published <i>Der Kampf der Theile im Organismus</i> (<i>The Struggle of Parts in the Organism</i>) in which he suggested that the development of an organism results from a Darwinian competition between the parts of the embryo, occurring at all levels, from molecules to organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> In recent years, a modern version of this theory has been proposed by <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Kupiec" title="fr:Jean-Jacques Kupiec">Jean-Jacques Kupiec</a>. According to this cellular Darwinism, <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">random variation</a> at the molecular level generates diversity in cell types whereas cell interactions impose a characteristic order on the developing embryo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_psychological_theory">Social and psychological theory</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology" title="Evolutionary psychology">Evolutionary psychology</a></div>
<p>The social implications of the theory of evolution by natural selection also became the source of continuing controversy. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>, a German <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosopher</a> and co-originator of the ideology of <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a>, wrote in 1872 that "Darwin did not know what a bitter satire he wrote on mankind, and especially on his countrymen, when he showed that free competition, the struggle for existence, which the economists celebrate as the highest historical achievement, is the normal state of the <i>animal kingdom</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> Herbert Spencer and the eugenics advocate <a href="/wiki/Francis_Galton" title="Francis Galton">Francis Galton</a>'s interpretation of natural selection as necessarily progressive, leading to supposed advances in intelligence and civilisation, became a justification for <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">social Darwinism</a>. For example, in 1940, <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Lorenz" title="Konrad Lorenz">Konrad Lorenz</a>, in writings that he subsequently disowned, used the theory as a justification for policies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> state. He wrote "... selection for toughness, heroism, and social utility ... must be accomplished by some human institution, if mankind, in default of selective factors, is not to be ruined by domestication-induced degeneracy. The racial idea as the basis of our state has already accomplished much in this respect."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> Others have developed ideas that human societies and culture <a href="/wiki/Sociocultural_evolution" title="Sociocultural evolution">evolve</a> by mechanisms analogous to those that apply to evolution of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>More recently, work among anthropologists and psychologists has led to the development of <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology" title="Sociobiology">sociobiology</a> and later of evolutionary psychology, a field that attempts to explain features of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">human psychology</a> in terms of adaptation to the ancestral environment. The most prominent example of evolutionary psychology, notably advanced in the early work of <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a> and later by <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a>, is the hypothesis that the human brain has adapted to <a href="/wiki/Language_acquisition" title="Language acquisition">acquire</a> the <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammatical</a> rules of <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinker_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinker-128">[127]</a></sup> Other aspects of human behaviour and social structures, from specific cultural norms such as <a href="/wiki/Westermarck_effect#Westermarck_effect" title="Westermarck effect">incest avoidance</a> to broader patterns such as <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">gender roles</a>, have been hypothesised to have similar origins as adaptations to the early environment in which modern humans evolved. By analogy to the action of natural selection on genes, the concept of <a href="/wiki/Meme" title="Meme">memes</a>—"units of cultural transmission," or culture's equivalents of genes undergoing selection and recombination—has arisen, first described in this form by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> in 1976<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> and subsequently expanded upon by philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> as explanations for complex cultural activities, including human <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Information_and_systems_theory">Information and systems theory</span></h3>
<p>In 1922, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_J._Lotka" title="Alfred J. Lotka">Alfred J. Lotka</a> proposed that natural selection might be understood as a physical principle that could be described in terms of the use of energy by a system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> a concept later developed by <a href="/wiki/Howard_T._Odum" title="Howard T. Odum">Howard T. Odum</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Maximum_power_principle" title="Maximum power principle">maximum power principle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a>, whereby evolutionary systems with selective advantage maximise the rate of useful energy transformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>The principles of natural selection have inspired a variety of computational techniques, such as "soft" <a href="/wiki/Artificial_life" title="Artificial life">artificial life</a>, that simulate selective processes and can be highly efficient in 'adapting' entities to an environment defined by a specified <a href="/wiki/Fitness_function" title="Fitness function">fitness function</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> For example, a class of <a href="/wiki/Heuristic" title="Heuristic">heuristic</a> <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_optimization" title="Mathematical optimization">optimisation</a> <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Genetic_algorithm" title="Genetic algorithm">genetic algorithms</a>, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/John_Henry_Holland" title="John Henry Holland">John Henry Holland</a> in the 1970s and expanded upon by <a href="/wiki/David_E._Goldberg" title="David E. Goldberg">David E. Goldberg</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> identify optimal solutions by simulated reproduction and mutation of a population of solutions defined by an initial <a href="/wiki/Probability_distribution" title="Probability distribution">probability distribution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> Such algorithms are particularly useful when applied to problems whose <a href="/wiki/Energy_landscape" title="Energy landscape">energy landscape</a> is very rough or has many local minima.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction">In fiction</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_in_fiction" title="Evolution in fiction">Evolution in fiction</a></div>
<p>Darwinian evolution by natural selection is pervasive in literature, whether taken optimistically in terms of how humanity may evolve towards perfection, or pessimistically in terms of the dire consequences of the interaction of human nature and the struggle for survival. Among major responses is <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Butler_(novelist)" title="Samuel Butler (novelist)">Samuel Butler</a>'s 1872 pessimistic <i><a href="/wiki/Erewhon" title="Erewhon">Erewhon</a></i> ("nowhere", written mostly backwards). In 1893 <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> imagined "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Man_of_the_Year_Million" title="The Man of the Year Million">The Man of the Year Million</a>", transformed by natural selection into a being with a huge head and eyes, and shrunken body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFE_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFE-138">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a>, a female animal making a choice of mate may be argued to be intending to get the best mate; there is no suggestion that she has any intention to improve the bloodline in the manner of an animal breeder.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
